    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41438"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Hybrid de novo genome assembly of red gromwell ( Lithospermum erythrorhizon ) reveals evolutionary insight into shikonin biosynthesis"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Horticulture Research 2020 7:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-06-01"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Lithospermum erythrorhizon (red gromwell; zicao) is a medicinal and economically valuable plant belonging to the Boraginaceae family. Roots from L. erythrorhizon have been used for centuries based on the antiviral and wound-healing properties produced from the bioactive compound shikonin and its derivatives. More recently, shikonin, its enantiomer alkannin, and several other shikonin/alkannin derivatives have collectively emerged as valuable natural colorants and as novel drug scaffolds. Despite several transcriptomes and proteomes having been generated from L. erythrorhizon, a reference genome is still unavailable. This has limited investigations into elucidating the shikonin/alkannin pathway and understanding its evolutionary and ecological significance. In this study, we obtained a de novo genome assembly for L. erythrorhizon using a combination of Oxford Nanopore long-read and Illumina short-read sequencing technologies. The resulting genome is &#8764;367.41&#8201;Mb long, with a contig N50 size of 314.31&#8201;kb and 27,720 predicted protein-coding genes. Using the L. erythrorhizon genome, we identified several additional p-hydroxybenzoate:geranyltransferase (PGT) homologs and provide insight into their evolutionary history. Phylogenetic analysis of prenyltransferases suggests that PGTs originated in a common ancestor of modern shikonin/alkannin-producing Boraginaceous species, likely from a retrotransposition-derived duplication event of an ancestral prenyltransferase gene. Furthermore, knocking down expression of LePGT1 in L. erythrorhizon hairy root lines revealed that LePGT1 is predominantly responsible for shikonin production early in culture establishment. Taken together, the reference genome reported in this study and the provided analysis on the evolutionary origin of shikonin/alkannin biosynthesis will guide elucidation of the remainder of the pathway."/>

    <meta name="description" content="Lithospermum erythrorhizon (red gromwell; zicao) is a medicinal and economically valuable plant belonging to the Boraginaceae family. Roots from L. erythrorhizon have been used for centuries based on the antiviral and wound-healing properties produced from the bioactive compound shikonin and its derivatives. More recently, shikonin, its enantiomer alkannin, and several other shikonin/alkannin derivatives have collectively emerged as valuable natural colorants and as novel drug scaffolds. Despite several transcriptomes and proteomes having been generated from L. erythrorhizon, a reference genome is still unavailable. This has limited investigations into elucidating the shikonin/alkannin pathway and understanding its evolutionary and ecological significance. In this study, we obtained a de novo genome assembly for L. erythrorhizon using a combination of Oxford Nanopore long-read and Illumina short-read sequencing technologies. The resulting genome is &#8764;367.41&#8201;Mb long, with a contig N50 size of 314.31&#8201;kb and 27,720 predicted protein-coding genes. Using the L. erythrorhizon genome, we identified several additional p-hydroxybenzoate:geranyltransferase (PGT) homologs and provide insight into their evolutionary history. Phylogenetic analysis of prenyltransferases suggests that PGTs originated in a common ancestor of modern shikonin/alkannin-producing Boraginaceous species, likely from a retrotransposition-derived duplication event of an ancestral prenyltransferase gene. Furthermore, knocking down expression of LePGT1 in L. erythrorhizon hairy root lines revealed that LePGT1 is predominantly responsible for shikonin production early in culture establishment. Taken together, the reference genome reported in this study and the provided analysis on the evolutionary origin of shikonin/alkannin biosynthesis will guide elucidation of the remainder of the pathway."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Lithospermum erythrorhizon</i> (red gromwell; zicao) is a medicinal and economically valuable plant belonging to the Boraginaceae family. Roots from <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> have been used for centuries based on the antiviral and wound-healing properties produced from the bioactive compound shikonin and its derivatives. More recently, shikonin, its enantiomer alkannin, and several other shikonin/alkannin derivatives have collectively emerged as valuable natural colorants and as novel drug scaffolds. Despite several transcriptomes and proteomes having been generated from <i>L. erythrorhizon</i>, a reference genome is still unavailable. This has limited investigations into elucidating the shikonin/alkannin pathway and understanding its evolutionary and ecological significance. In this study, we obtained a de novo genome assembly for <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> using a combination of Oxford Nanopore long-read and Illumina short-read sequencing technologies. The resulting genome is <span class="stix">â¼</span>367.41âMb long, with a contig N50 size of 314.31âkb and 27,720 predicted protein-coding genes. Using the <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genome, we identified several additional <i><u>p</u></i>-hydroxybenzoate:<u>g</u>eranyl<u>t</u>ransferase (PGT) homologs and provide insight into their evolutionary history. Phylogenetic analysis of prenyltransferases suggests that PGTs originated in a common ancestor of modern shikonin/alkannin-producing Boraginaceous species, likely from a retrotransposition-derived duplication event of an ancestral prenyltransferase gene. Furthermore, knocking down expression of <i>LePGT1</i> in <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> hairy root lines revealed that LePGT1 is predominantly responsible for shikonin production early in culture establishment. Taken together, the reference genome reported in this study and the provided analysis on the evolutionary origin of shikonin/alkannin biosynthesis will guide elucidation of the remainder of the pathway.</p></div></div></section>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The purple-colored roots of red gromwell (<i>Lithospermum erythrorhizon</i>; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig1">1a, b</a>), also known as âzicaoâ in Chinese, âjichiâ in Korean, and âmurasakiâ in Japanese, have been used as part of traditional medicines, as a dyestuff, and in cosmetics across many cultures for centuries. The responsible bioactive and pigmented compounds, shikoninâor its enantiomer, alkanninâand dozens of other acylated shikonin/alkannin derivatives (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig1">1c</a>), are synthesized in the root periderm of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> and several other Boraginaceae species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Papageorgiou, V. P., Assimopoulou, A. N., Couladouros, E. A., Hepworth, D. &amp; Nicolaou, K. C. The chemistry and biology of alkannin, shikonin, and related naphthazarin natural products. Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. 38, 270â300 (1999)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d65884e569">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Skoneczny, D. et al. Metabolic profiling and identification of shikonins in root periderm of two invasive Echium spp. weeds in Australia. Molecules 22, 330 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d65884e572">2</a></sup>. Shikonins/alkannins are deposited into the rhizosphere where they function in plant-microbe interactions and interfere with the growth of competing plants (allelopathy), roles suggested to have contributed to the invasion success of species like <i>Echium plantagineum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Zhu, X. et al. Identification and localization of bioactive naphthoquinones in the roots and rhizosphere of Patersonâs curse (Echium plantagineum), a noxious invader. J. Exp. Bot. 67, 3777â3788 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d65884e579">3</a></sup>. The presence of alkannins was also reported in <i>Plagiobothrys arizonicus</i> leaves<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schmid, H. V. &amp; Zenk, M. H. p-hydroxybenzoic acid and mevalonic acid as precursors of the plant naphthoquinone alkannin. Tetrahedron Lett. 12, 4151â4155 (1971)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d65884e586">4</a></sup>, though the physiological and/or ecological significance of their presence in aerial tissues is unclear.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Shikonin is produced in the roots of Lithospermum erythrorhizon."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Shikonin is produced in the roots of <i>Lithospermum erythrorhizon</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0301-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_301_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0301-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_301_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="888" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b> Intact roots of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> producing shikonin. <b>b</b> Hairy root culture of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> producing shikonin. <b>c</b> The structures of shikonin and its enantiomer, alkannin. Shikonin and alkannin are precursor to dozens of acylated derivatives collectively produced by members of the Boraginaceae. <b>d</b> The shikonin pathway starts with the conjugation of 4-hydroxybenzoic acid (4HBA) and geranyl diphosphate (GPP) catalyzed by <i>p</i>-hydroxybenzoate:geranyltransferase (PGT)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Shikonins/alkannins have more recently been discovered to exhibit a range of pharmacological properties<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Widhalm, J. R. &amp; Rhodes, D. Biosynthesis and molecular actions of specialized 1,4-naphthoquinone natural products produced by horticultural plants. Hortic. Res 3, 16046 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d65884e638">5</a></sup>. Shikonin has been found to suppress human immunodeficiency virus (HIV) type 1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Chen, X. et al. Shikonin, a component of Chinese herbal medicine, inhibits chemokine receptor function and suppresses human immunodeficiency virus type 1. Society 47, 2810â2816 (2003)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d65884e642">6</a></sup> and to display anti-tumor effects in breast cancer cells via multiple signaling pathways<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Duru, N., Gernapudi, R. &amp; Zhou, Q. Chemopreventive activities of shikonin in breast cancer. Biochem Pharm. 3, e163 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d65884e646">7</a></sup>. Thus, combined with their traditional medicinal and cosmetic value, there has been wide interest for many decades in scaling shikonin/alkannin production. Early efforts back in the 1970s and 1980s centered on producing shikonin in <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> cell cultures, which was also the first industrial scale platform for producing a secondary metabolite in dedifferentiated plant cells<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Yazaki, K. Lithospermum erythrorhizon cell cultures: present and future aspects. Plant Biotechnol. 34, 131â142 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d65884e653">8</a></sup>. With advances in understanding of pathway precursors<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schmid, H. V. &amp; Zenk, M. H. p-hydroxybenzoic acid and mevalonic acid as precursors of the plant naphthoquinone alkannin. Tetrahedron Lett. 12, 4151â4155 (1971)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d65884e658">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Gaisser, S. &amp; Heide, L. Inhibition and regulation of shikonin biosynthesis in suspension cultures of Lithospermum. Phytochemistry 41, 1065â1072 (1996)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d65884e661">9</a></sup>, strategies to increase shikonin production in <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> through metabolic engineering were developed (e.g.,<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Boehm, R., Sommer, S., Li, S. M. &amp; Heide, L. Genetic engineering on shikonin biosynthesis: expression of the bacterial ubiA gene in Lithospermum erythrorhizon. Plant Cell Physiol. 41, 911â919 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d65884e668">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="KÃ¶hle, A. et al. High level expression of chorismate pyruvate-lyase (UbiC) and HMG-CoA reductase in hairy root cultures of Lithospermum erythrorhizon. Plant Cell Physiol. 43, 894â902 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d65884e671">11</a></sup>). Current efforts have extended to include synthetic chemistry for producing shikonin, alkannin, and derivatives with higher specificity and potency<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Wang, R. et al. An efficient multigram synthesis of alkannin and shikonin. Eur. J. Org. Chem. 2012, 1373â1379 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d65884e675">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wang, F., Yao, X., Zhang, Y. &amp; Tang, J. Synthesis, biological function and evaluation of Shikonin in cancer therapy. Fitoterapia 134, 329â339 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d65884e678">13</a></sup>. Moreover, the use of comparative transcriptomics (e.g.,<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, S., Ping, L. &amp; Teng, G. Different secondary metabolic responses to MeJA treatment in shikonin-proficient and shikonin-deficient cell lines from Arnebia euchroma (Royle) Johnst. Plant Cell Tiss. Org. 119, 587â598 (2014)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d65884e682">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhu, Y. et al. Involvement of LeMDR, an ATP-binding cassette protein gene, in shikonin transport and biosynthesis in Lithospermum erythrorhizon. BMC Plant Biol. 17, 1â10. (2017)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d65884e682_1">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wu, F.-Y. et al. Transcriptome analysis explores genes related to shikonin biosynthesis in Lithospermeae plants and provides insights into Boraginales? evolutionary history. Sci. Rep. 7, 4477 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d65884e685">16</a></sup>) and proteomics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Takanashi, K. et al. Comparative proteomic analysis of Lithospermum erythrorhizon reveals regulation of a variety of metabolic enzymes leading to comprehensive understanding of the shikonin biosynthetic pathway. Plant Cell Physiol. 60, 19â28 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d65884e689">17</a></sup> approaches for elucidating the shikonin/alkannin pathway has uncovered several gene candidates and led to the identification of the geranylhydroquinone hydroxylase (GHQH; CYP76B74)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Wang, S. et al. CYP76B74 catalyzes the 3â-hydroxylation of geranylhydroquinone in shikonin biosynthesis. Plant Physiol. 179, 402â414 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d65884e694">18</a></sup>. Comparatively less attention has focused on the evolutionary origin of shikonin/alkannin pathway genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wu, F.-Y. et al. Transcriptome analysis explores genes related to shikonin biosynthesis in Lithospermeae plants and provides insights into Boraginales? evolutionary history. Sci. Rep. 7, 4477 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d65884e698">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Rai, A. et al. De novo transcriptome assembly and characterization of lithospermum officinale to discover putative genes involved in specialized metabolites biosynthesis. Planta Med. 84, 320â934. (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d65884e701">19</a></sup> and on the ecological significance of producing the compounds<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Zhu, X. et al. Identification and localization of bioactive naphthoquinones in the roots and rhizosphere of Patersonâs curse (Echium plantagineum), a noxious invader. J. Exp. Bot. 67, 3777â3788 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d65884e705">3</a></sup>. It was only recently that the <i>E. plantagineum</i> genome was published, the first from a shikonin/alkannin-producing species or a member of the Boraginales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Tang, C., Li, S., Wang, Y.-T. &amp; Wang, X. Comparative genome/transcriptome analysis probes Boraginalesâ phylogenetic position, WGDs in Boraginales, and key enzyme genes in the alkannin/shikonin core pathway. Mol. Ecol. Resour. 20, 228â241 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d65884e712">20</a></sup>.</p><p>Despite being among the longest-studied plant natural products, there are still many gaps in knowledge about shikonin/alkannin pathway genes, architecture, and regulation. In this study, we report the first de novo genome for <i>L. erythrorhizon</i>, ï»¿generated by combining Oxford Nanopore Technology (ONT) long reads with Illumina HiSeq short reads. From this assembly, we identified the existence of 11 previously unreported <i><u>p</u></i>-hydroxybenzoate:<u>g</u>eranyl<u>t</u>ransferase (PGT; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig1">1c</a>) homologs and provide insight into their contribution to shikonin biosynthesis based on (i) their distribution amongst shikonin/alkannin-producing species within the Boraginaceae and (ii) <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> knock-down hairy root lines with reduced expression of <i>LePGT1</i>. Taken together, the results of our study provide evolutionary insight into the origin of the shikonin/alkannin pathway, and the genome assembly offers a major resource for exploring outstanding questions in shikonin/alkannin metabolism.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and annotation</h3><p>To create a reference genome, we combined <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> ONT genomic DNA (gDNA) reads generated in-house from Siebold &amp; Zucc. plants with publicly available Illumina gDNA reads sequenced by Nanjing University in 2018 from an unknown accession (SRR5644206). The Illumina data consisted of ~21.7âGb Illumina HiSeq paired-end short reads (150âbp) with an estimated heterozygosity of 0.39% and projected genome size of 369.34âMb (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM1">1a</a>). Our in-house ONT data consisted of ~7.6âGb long-reads (N50â=â15.03âkb) providing roughly 20-fold genome coverage. The short and long reads assembled into 2465 contigs using the DBG2OLC hybrid assembler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ye, C., Hill, C. M., Wu, S., Ruan, J. &amp; Ma, Z. DBG2OLC: Efficient assembly of large genomes using long erroneous reads of the third generation sequencing technologies. Sci. Rep. 6, 1â9 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d65884e759">21</a></sup>, yielding a 367.41âMb genome with a longest contig of 3.44âMb and an N50 contig length of 314.31âkb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Summary of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genome assembly and gene models</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Using a de novo repeat modeler, 51.78% of assembly bases were denoted as repetitive elements and were subsequently masked (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">1</a>). Of these elements, the majority were long terminal repeats (LTRs) which comprised 23.43% of the genome assembly. Unclassified elements were the second most common, accounting for 21% of the genome. DNA repeat elements comprised 4.45% of the genome. The repeat content in <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> is comparable to the repeat content observed in the other sequenced Boraginaceae, <i>E. plantagineum</i> (43.3% repetitive; 23.08% LTRs)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Tang, C., Li, S., Wang, Y.-T. &amp; Wang, X. Comparative genome/transcriptome analysis probes Boraginalesâ phylogenetic position, WGDs in Boraginales, and key enzyme genes in the alkannin/shikonin core pathway. Mol. Ecol. Resour. 20, 228â241 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1010">20</a></sup>.</p><p>Protein-coding genes were identified through a combination of ab initio, homology-based, and transcriptome-based prediction methods. A total of 27,720 genes encoding 39,395 proteins were predicted (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Tab1">1</a>). The average protein-coding gene was 3772âbp long and contained 7 exons. Functional annotations were assigned to 80.02%, 72.89%, 59.30%, 23.57%, 7.53%, 5.85% of genes using the InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236â1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1020">22</a></sup>, Pfam<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="El-Gebali, S., et al. The Pfam protein families database in 2019. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gky995&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1024">23</a></sup>, GO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Gene Ontology Consortium. The Gene Ontology (GO) database and informatics resource. Nucleic Acids Res 32, 258Dâ261D (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1028">24</a></sup>, Trans Membrane (TMHMM), KEGG, and MetaCyc<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Caspi, R., et al. The MetaCyc database of metabolic pathways and enzymes. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkx935&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1032">25</a></sup> databases, respectively (Supplementary Dataset S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM8">1</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Quality assessment</h3><p>To evaluate the completeness and coverage of the assembly, we aligned the ONT gDNA, Illumina gDNA, and Illumina RNA-seq reads to the <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genome assembly. Coverage histograms of the ONT and Illumina gDNA reads showed a single peak at ~18-fold and ~45-fold coverage, respectively (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM1">1b, c</a>), indicative of the genome being largely homozygous. The alignment rates of the Illumina gDNA reads was high (95.97%); however 46% of the assembled genome lacked gDNA read support (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM1">1c</a>). This is likely due to the Illumina gDNA reads being generated via a PCR-amplified library, leading to inconsistent coverage across the genome. However, the amount of coding-regions with Illumina gDNA read support was 89.6% (â¥10 mapped reads), allowing for sufficient base corrections of the ONT reads in the genic regions, which are the focus of our study. The alignment rate of the RNA reads ranged from 59.29% to 72.71% (in the case of libraries prepared via ribosomal depletion) to 86.74% to 90.15% (for libraries prepared via polyA capture) (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">2</a>).</p><p>We then used BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Waterhouse, R. M. et al. BUSCO applications from quality assessments to gene prediction and phylogenomics. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 543â548 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1063">26</a></sup> to assess the completeness of the predicted proteome. Within the <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> protein-coding gene set, 1142 of 1400 conserved embryophyta genes (79.3%) were identified as complete, of those 93.43% were present in single-copy and 6.57% were duplicated (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">3</a>). Furthermore, 279 of 303 conserved eukaryota genes (92.08%) were identified as complete, of those 78.14% were present in single-copy and 21.86% were duplicated (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">3</a>). Lastly, we calculated the Alien Index (AI) for all predicted proteins of the genome assembly to assess possible contamination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wisecaver, J. H., Alexander, W. G., King, S. B., Todd Hittinger, C., Rokas, A. Dynamic evolution of nitric oxide detoxifying flavohemoglobins, a family of single-protein metabolic modules in bacteria and eukaryotes. Mol. Biol. Evol. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msw073&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1076">27</a></sup>. No assembly contig had a majority of their predicted proteins with AI scores &gt;0, indicating no detectible contamination in the assembly. Only 50 of the 39,395 total proteins (0.13%) had AI scores &gt;0.05, which could be indicative of horizontal gene transfer (HGT) (Supplementary Dataset S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM9">2</a>). However, manual inspection of each of these proteins did not yield any strong HGT candidates.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene family analysis</h3><p>To investigate the evolution of different gene families, including those involved in the production of shikonin, we performed an OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 1â14. (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1092">28</a></sup> analysis using the protein-coding genes of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> and 31 other eudicot species (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">4</a>). Incorporated into our analysis were four additional Boraginaceae species, including three species known to produce shikonin (<i>E. plantagineum, Arnebia euchroma</i>, and <i>Lithospermum officinale</i>) as well as <i>Mertensia paniculate</i>, a Boraginaceae whose transcriptome was sequenced by the 1000 Plants Initiative (oneKP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Leebens-Mack, J. H. et al. One thousand plant transcriptomes and the phylogenomics of green plants. Nature &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-019-1693-2&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1112">29</a></sup>) and whose ability to produce shikonin is unknown. We also included 14 additional Boraginales species sequenced by the oneKP project (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">4</a>). Our OrthoFinder-inferred species tree had the Boraginales sister to a large clade consisting of the Solanales, Gentianales, and Laminales (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig2">2</a>). This placement is in disagreement with the analysis by Tang et al<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Tang, C., Li, S., Wang, Y.-T. &amp; Wang, X. Comparative genome/transcriptome analysis probes Boraginalesâ phylogenetic position, WGDs in Boraginales, and key enzyme genes in the alkannin/shikonin core pathway. Mol. Ecol. Resour. 20, 228â241 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1122">20</a></sup>. that showed the Boraginales sister to the Solanales. This conflict is unsurprising as the evolutionary relationships of these lamiid orders remains uncertain<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Chase. M. W. et al. An update of the Angiosperm Phylogeny Group classification for the orders and families of flowering plants: APG IV. Bot. J. Linn. Soc. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/boj.12385&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1126">30</a></sup>. Additional work is needed to resolve these relationships and determine the source of the phylogenetic discordance.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="OrthoFinder gene family analysis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: OrthoFinder gene family analysis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0301-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_301_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0301-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_301_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="762" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>The OrthoFinder inferred species phylogeny is displayed on the left. The branch thickness is scaled based on the number of predicted duplicated events to have occurred at the descendent node; thinner branches indicate fewer duplications, thicker branches indicate more (see Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">7</a>). Internodes discussed in the text (N1, N5, N10, and N15) are labeled (the species tree with all labeled internodes can be accessed in Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">7</a>). Asterisks (*) indicate known shikonin/alkannin-producing species. Horizontal bar plots (right) indicate the number of orthogroups that are species-specific (red), maintained in all 32 species (green), or present in more than one but less than all species in the analysis (blue)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The OrthoFinder analysis identified 100,874 orthogroups (predicted gene families), of which 24,346 consisted of two or more species in the analysis (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">5</a>). Of the 14,885 orthogroups containing one or more <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> sequences, 3441 orthogroups (23.13%) were present in all species, 3003 (20.17%) were <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> specific, and 8441 (56.71%) consisted of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> and one or more additional species (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig2">2</a>; Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">6</a>). In total, 36,392 of 39,395 <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> proteins (92.38%) were assigned to an orthogroup containing sequence(s) from one or more additional species (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">6</a>; Supplementary Dataset S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM10">3</a>). The total number of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> orthogroups (14,885) was comparable to the other sequenced genomes in the analysis (average 15,227), which ranged from 12,666 in <i>Solanum lycopersicum</i> (tomato) to 19,349 in <i>Actinidia chinensis</i> (kiwi). Furthermore, the percentage of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> orthogroups that was species-specific (20.17%) was also comparable to the other sequenced genomes (average 22.47%), ranging from 5.05% in tomato to 46.55% in <i>Utricularia gibba</i> (a carnivorous aquatic bladderwort). The other three species known to produce shikonin showed an increased number of orthogroups (average 26,040.33), which is likely a result of their predicted proteomes being derived from de novo transcriptome assemblies rather than sequenced genomes. The average percentage of species-specific orthogroups found in the oneKP transcriptomes (0.50%) was noticeably lower that the other species in the analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig2">2</a>) due to these proteomes being filtered prior to publication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Leebens-Mack, J. H. et al. One thousand plant transcriptomes and the phylogenomics of green plants. Nature &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/s41586-019-1693-2&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1207">29</a></sup>.</p><p>To identify orthogroups that had expanded in one or more ancestors of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i>, we parsed the number of OrthoFinder-predicted gene duplications at each node of the inferred species tree (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig2">2</a>; Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">7</a>). The average number of orthogroups that duplicated one or more times at a given internode (i.e., non-leaf node) was 1,489.55 and ranged from 41 duplications at internode N5 (the common ancestor of <i>Daucus carota</i> and <i>A. chinensis)</i> to 4489 duplications at internode N1 (the common ancestor of Caryophyllales and asterids) (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">7</a>). A total of 2818 orthogroups duplicated at internode N10 (the common ancestor of the five Boraginaceae species), and the <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genes that duplicated at this internode were enriched in 21 gene ontology (GO) categories (BenjaminiâHochberg adjusted <i>p-</i>value &lt;â0.1; Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">8</a>) including transferase activity (GO:0016740; <i>p</i>â=â1.10eâ3), signal transduction (GO:0007165; <i>p</i>â=â6.45eâ4), and transmembrane transporter activity (GO:0022857; <i>p</i>â=â0.08). Similarly, the <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genes from the 3908 orthogroups that duplicated at internode N15 (the common ancestor of the four Boraginaceae species known to produce shikonin) were enriched in 14 GO categories (BenjaminiâHochberg adjusted <i>p-</i>valueâ&lt;â0.1; Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">8</a>) including transferase activity of acyl groups (GO:0016746; <i>p</i>â=â0.3), DNA-binding transcription factor activity (GO:0003700; <i>p</i>â=â3.79eâ3), and transmembrane transport (GO:0055085; <i>p</i>â=â0.07).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Evolution of p-hydroxybenzoate:geranyltransferase (PGT) genes for shikonin biosynthesis</h3><p>One orthogroup predicted to have undergone multiple duplication events in the last common ancestor of shikonin/alkannin-producing species was OG0000509. This orthogroup was comprised of genes that code for prenyltransferases, including the characterized ubiquinone prenyltransferase in <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, AtPPT1 (Coq2; At4g23660)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Okada, K. et al. The AtPPT1 gene encoding 4-hydroxybenzoate polyprenyl diphosphate transferase in ubiquinone biosynthesis is required for embryo development in Arabidopsis thaliana. Plant Mol. Biol. 55, 567â577 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1282">31</a></sup>. In addition, OG0000509 contained 13 <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genes (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">9</a>), including Leryth_015068 (hereafter referred to as <i>LePGT1</i>), which encodes a protein 97.06% identical to LePGT1, and Leryth_002561 (hereafter referred to as <i>LePGT2</i>), which encodes a protein 100% identical to LePGT2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yazaki, K., Kunihisa, M., Fujisaki, T. &amp; Sato, F. Geranyl diphosphate:4-hydroxybenzoate geranyltransferase from Lithospermum erythrorhizon: cloning and characterization of a key enzyme in shikonin biosynthesis. J. Biol. Chem. 277, 6240â6246 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1299">32</a></sup>. The <i>p</i>-hydroxybenzoate:geranyltransferases (PGTs) catalyze the conjugation of 4-hydroxybenzoic acid (4HBA) and geranyl diphosphate (GPP), the first and committed step of the shikonin pathway (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig1">1d</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yazaki, K., Kunihisa, M., Fujisaki, T. &amp; Sato, F. Geranyl diphosphate:4-hydroxybenzoate geranyltransferase from Lithospermum erythrorhizon: cloning and characterization of a key enzyme in shikonin biosynthesis. J. Biol. Chem. 277, 6240â6246 (2002)." href="#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1309">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Yazaki, K., Fukui, H. &amp; Tabata, M. Isolation of the intermediates and related metabolites of shikonin biosynthesis from Lithospermum erythrorhizon cell cultures. Chem. Pharm. Bull. (Tokyo) 34, 2290â2293 (1986)." href="#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1309_1">33</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Heide, L. &amp; Tabata, M. Geranylpyrophosphate: p-hydroxybenzoate geranyltransferase activity in extracts of Lithospermum erythrorhizon cell cultures. Phytochemistry 26, 1651â1655 (1987)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1312">34</a></sup> and have been identified from several shikonin/alkannin-producing species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Rai, A. et al. De novo transcriptome assembly and characterization of lithospermum officinale to discover putative genes involved in specialized metabolites biosynthesis. Planta Med. 84, 320â934. (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1316">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Singh, R. S. et al. Expression of 3-hydroxy-3-methylglutaryl-CoA reductase, p-hydroxybenzoate-m-geranyltransferase and genes of phenylpropanoid pathway exhibits positive correlation with shikonins content in arnebia [Arnebia euchroma (Royle) Johnston]. BMC Mol. Biol. 11, 88 (2010)." href="#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1319">35</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wu, S. J. S.-J. et al. Nitric oxide regulates shikonin formation in suspension-cultured onosma paniculatum cells. Plant Cell Physiol. 50, 118â128 (2009)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1319_1">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wu, F. Y. et al. Transcriptome analysis explores genes related to shikonin biosynthesis in Lithospermeae plants and provides insights into Boraginalesâ evolutionary history. Sci Rep. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/s41598-017-04750-1&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1322">37</a></sup>.</p><p>To better understand the evolutionary history of LePGT1 and LePGT2, we constructed a robust phylogenetic tree of OG0000509 prenyltransferases (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig3">3a</a>). Of the 13 sequences in <i>L. erythrorhizon</i>, one was excluded from the phylogenetic analysis due to it being a suspected pseudogene (Leryth_015069), which is located ~10âkb away from <i>LePGT1</i> and is likely the result of a tandem duplication event (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM2">2</a>). Pseudogenization is suspected due to the fact that the sequence appears to be truncated and is missing the conserved NDXXD motif indicative of putative prenyl diphosphate binding (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM3">3</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yazaki, K., Kunihisa, M., Fujisaki, T. &amp; Sato, F. Geranyl diphosphate:4-hydroxybenzoate geranyltransferase from Lithospermum erythrorhizon: cloning and characterization of a key enzyme in shikonin biosynthesis. J. Biol. Chem. 277, 6240â6246 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1345">32</a></sup>. In agreement with the OrthoFinder analysis, the phylogeny shows a large radiation of prenyltransferase genes in the Boraginales followed by an additional radiation in the Boraginaceae. The Leryth_011786 gene copy is notable in that it is on a small branch relative to the other <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> sequences and groups closest to homologs in the other lamiids (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig3">3a</a>), suggesting it is likely the âmissingâ ubiquinone prenyltransferase<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Kusano H., et al. Evolutionary developments in plant specialized metabolism, exemplified by two transferase families. Front Plant Sci. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2019.00794&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1355">38</a></sup> (hereafter referred to LePPT1).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Phylogenetic analysis of prenyltransferase homologs in orthogroup OG0000509."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Phylogenetic analysis of prenyltransferase homologs in orthogroup OG0000509.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0301-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_301_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0301-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_301_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="623" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b> Maximum likelihood tree of orthogroup OG0000509 rooted on <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> ubiquinone polyprenyltransferase, AtPPT1. Nodes with IQ-TREE ultrafast parametric support values &gt;0.95 are indicated by gray circles on the preceding branch. The branches and outer color bar are color-coded to match the taxonomic classification of each sequence. <i>Lithospermum erythrorhizon</i> sequences are indicated by the red font. The hypothetical location of the inferred duplication via retrotransposition in the ancestor of genes encoding PGTs and PGT-like homologs is indicated by the gray arrow. <b>b</b> Heatmap showing the gene expression pattern of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> prenyltransferase genes in whole roots (R), aerial tissue (A), root periderm (P), root vascular (V), hairy root grown in the dark (D), and hairy root grown in the light (L). Conditions where shikonin is most abundant are indicated with an aterisk (*). The cladogram (left) shows the evolutionary relationship between prenyltransferase genes according to the overall maximum likelihood phylogeny in part a</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>As recognized by Kusano et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Kusano H., et al. Evolutionary developments in plant specialized metabolism, exemplified by two transferase families. Front Plant Sci. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.3389/fpls.2019.00794&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1396">38</a></sup>, <i>LePGT1</i> and <i>LePGT2</i> are both comprised of a single exon (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM2">2</a>). The six <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genes most closely related to <i>LePGT1</i> and <i>LePGT2</i> (Leryth_008226, Leryth_027669, Leryth_027448, Leryth_000057, Leryth_020476, Leryth_003298) are also single exonic. In contrast, <i>AtPPT1</i>, as well as its predicted <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> ortholog, <i>LePPT1</i>, are multi-exonic, each containing eight exons. The three remaining unclassified <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genes in the phylogeny were found to contain a variable number of exons: Leryth_023194 with ten exons, Leryth_011510 with eight exons, and Leryth_006101 with ten exons. The loss of exons in <i>LePGT1</i> and <i>LePGT2</i>, and the six additional <i>PGT-like</i> genes, suggests that a retrotranspotition event is responsible for the duplication that gave rise to the specialized prenyltransferase genes involved in the production of shikonin.</p><p>In order to investigate the mechanisms and timing of the PGT gene family expansion, we identified syntenic blocks for the entire <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genome assembly and performed synonymous substitution (Ks) pattern analyses. Regions of shared Ks values among homologous genes within syntenic blocks would be indicative of a possible whole-genome duplication (WGD) in the ancestor of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i>. We identified a (Ks) peak at roughly ~0.45, a value that is suggestive of a potential polyploidy event<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Lyons E., Freeling M. How to usefully compare homologous plant genes and chromosomes as DNA sequences. Plant J. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/j.1365-313X.2007.03326.x&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2008)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1450">39</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM4">S4a</a>), and roughly matches the results of Tang et al., who performed a larger analysis of WGD in the Boraginaceae. In the Tang analysis, the Ks peak of ~0.417 was proposed to have arisen via a WGD in the ancestor of the Boraginaceae roughly 25 MYA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Tang, C., Li, S., Wang, Y.-T. &amp; Wang, X. Comparative genome/transcriptome analysis probes Boraginalesâ phylogenetic position, WGDs in Boraginales, and key enzyme genes in the alkannin/shikonin core pathway. Mol. Ecol. Resour. 20, 228â241 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1457">20</a></sup>. Given evidence of a probable WGD in the ancestor of <i>L</i>. erythrorrhizon, the expansion of the OG0000509 genes could have arisen due to the WGD or subsequent duplication processes (e.g., tandem duplication, retrotransposition). However, only two syntenic blocks containing PGT and PGT-like genes were identified (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">10</a>). The first syntenic block had a median Ks of 0.534 and contained PGT1 (Leryth_015168) and another single exonic PGT-like gene (Leryth_008226) (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM4">4b</a>). The second syntenic block had a median Ks of 0.466 and contained two multi-exonic PGT-like genes (Leryth_011510 and Leryth_006101) (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM4">4c</a>). Given that the median Ks of the two syntenic blocks containing PGT and PGT-like genes are approximate to the multi-locus peak (~0.45) in the Ks distribution (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM4">4a</a>), it is possible that these duplications arose via the WGD event. Though, the fact that there is shared synteny between two multi-exonic homologs as well as between two single-exonic homologs, while no evidence of shared synteny between multi-exonic homologs and single-exonic homologs was observed, suggests that the putative retrotransposition event occurred prior to the whole-genome duplication. This apparent lack of synteny also indicates that the contigs harboring PGT homologs are not simply haplotypes resulting from the genome assembly process, but that these genes are found on contigs with unique neighboring genetic sequence.</p><p>Aside from the suspected pseudogene, all other encoded prenyltransferases in orthogroup OG0000509 contain the conserved NDXXD motif indicative of putative prenyl diphosphate binding (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM3">3</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yazaki, K., Kunihisa, M., Fujisaki, T. &amp; Sato, F. Geranyl diphosphate:4-hydroxybenzoate geranyltransferase from Lithospermum erythrorhizon: cloning and characterization of a key enzyme in shikonin biosynthesis. J. Biol. Chem. 277, 6240â6246 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1484">32</a></sup>. All but two unclassified homologs (Leryth_023194 and Leryth_006101) also maintained the GX(K/Y)STAL sequence motif conserved in this subfamily of 4HB:prenyltransferases (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM3">3</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yazaki, K., Kunihisa, M., Fujisaki, T. &amp; Sato, F. Geranyl diphosphate:4-hydroxybenzoate geranyltransferase from Lithospermum erythrorhizon: cloning and characterization of a key enzyme in shikonin biosynthesis. J. Biol. Chem. 277, 6240â6246 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1491">32</a></sup>. One of these unclassified homologs, Leryth_023194, also contained an N-terminal chloroplast targeting sequence (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">11</a>). None of the other prenyltransferase homologs contained a detectable signal or transit peptide. Lastly, we checked the relative gene expression of all prenyltransferase homologs in three tissue/growth condition comparisons in which shikonin levels and/or expression of shikonin pathway genes are variably abundant: <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> whole root tissue versus aboveground tissue<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wu, F. Y. et al. Transcriptome analysis explores genes related to shikonin biosynthesis in Lithospermeae plants and provides insights into Boraginalesâ evolutionary history. Sci Rep. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/s41598-017-04750-1&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1502">37</a></sup>, root outer periderm versus inner vascular tissue (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM5">5</a>), and hairy root tissue cultures grown in M9 in the dark versus in B5 in the light<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zhang, W. J. et al. Expression analysis of shikonin-biosynthetic genes in response to M9 medium and light in Lithospermum erythrorhizon cell cultures. Plant Cell Tissue Organ Cult. 101, 135â142 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1509">40</a></sup>. <i>LePGT1 and LePGT2</i>, along with <i>PGT-like</i> homologs Leryth_027448 and Leryth_027669 were significantly overrepresented (adjusted <i>p</i> value &lt; 0.05, log<sub>2</sub>-fold change &gt;1) in conditions associated with known increased shikonin production or shikonin pathway gene expression states (whole root tissue, root periderm tissue, and hairy root grown in M9 in the dark; Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig3">3b</a>; Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">9</a>). Additionally, <i>PGT-like</i> homolog Leryth_020476 was significantly overrepresented in whole root and root periderm tissue (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig3">3b</a>; Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">9</a>). Lastly, Leryth_000057 and Leyrth_023194 were significantly overrepresented in root periderm tissue. The remaining PGT homologs showed zero to low expression in all samples.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">LePGT1 is the predominant PGT functioning in the shikonin pathway</h3><p>LePGT1 is considered as the key regulatory enzyme in the shikonin pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Ohara, K., Muroya, A., Fukushima, N. &amp; Yazaki, K. Functional characterization of LePGT1, a membrane-bound prenyltransferase involved in the geranylation of p-hydroxybenzoic acid. Biochem J. 421, 231â241 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1550">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Ohara, K., Mito, K. &amp; Yazaki, K. Homogeneous purification and characterization of LePGT1âa membrane-bound aromatic substrate prenyltransferase involved in secondary metabolism of Lithospermum erythrorhizon. FEBS J. 280, 2572â2580 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1553">42</a></sup>. As there are no reported genetic studies with <i>PGTs</i>, and in light of the newly identified PGT-like encoding genes found in the <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig4">4</a>), we knocked down <i>LePGT1</i> expression in <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> hairy roots to investigate if LePGT1 is indeed the predominant PGT controlling shikonin production. Several independent <i>PGT1</i>-RNAi (<i>PGT1i</i>) lines were generated, excised, transferred to B5 media plates for selection, and then screened based on total shikonins (the sum of shikonin plus its derivatives) production in liquid culture using HPLC coupled with diode array detection (DAD). Individual lines were cultured in liquid B5 in constant light without selection for 14âd and then transferred to M9 and constant darkness to induce shikonin production. Analysis of culture media 3âd after transfer to M9 and darkness revealed 17 <i>PGT1i</i> lines producing between 1 and 59% of the total shikonins synthesized by control hairy roots sampled at the same time (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig4">4a</a>). Two lines, <i>PGT1i</i>-21 and <i>PGT1i</i>-79, were further analyzed by qRT-PCR. Both lines were found to have greater than 95% reduced <i>LePGT1</i> levels while expression of <i>LePGT2</i> remained statistically unchanged compared to the control (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig4">4b</a>). Because the gene encoding LePGT1 is more similar to LePGT2 than any of the PGT-likes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig3">3a</a>), these data indicate that the RNAi construct specifically targeted <i>LePGT1</i>. Analysis of the culture media from the same hairy roots used to perform qRT-PCR revealed that the reduction in <i>LePGT1</i> expression (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig4">4b</a>) correlated with a more than 95% decrease in total shikonin content (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig4">4c</a>). These results provide further support for LePGT1 being predominantly responsible for the formation of 3-geranyl-4HBA. They also imply that the PGT-like proteins encoded in the <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genome likely do not play a major role in shikonin production.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="In vivo characterization of LePGT1."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: In vivo characterization of LePGT1.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0301-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_301_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0301-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_301_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="461" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b> Screening of <i>LePGT1</i>-RNAi (<i>PGT1i</i>) lines based on total shikonin levels present in liquid culture media 3âd after transfer of 14-d-old hairy roots to M9 and darkness. <b>b</b> Expression levels of <i>LePGT1</i> and <i>LePGT2</i> in hairy roots of two independent <i>PGT1i</i> lines compared to control. <b>c</b> Analysis of total shikonin in same lines used to measure expression in panel <b>b</b>. All data are meansâÂ±âSEM (<i>n</i>â=â3â4 biological replicates). Different letters indicate significant differences via analysis of variance (ANOVA) followed by post-hoc Tukey test (Î±â=â0.05). In <b>b</b>, lowercase and capital letters correspond to statistical comparisons for <i>LePGT1</i> and <i>LePGT2</i> expression, respectively</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>In this study, we report the first de novo assembly of a <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genome obtained through a combination of ONT long-read and Illumina HiSeq short-read sequencing technologies. The ~367.41âMb assembly contained 27,720 predicted protein-coding genes that were clustered into 14,885 orthologous gene families, 79.84% of which were also found in other species in our comparative phylogenetic analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig2">2</a>). A significant number of these orthogroups (2818) appear to have duplicated in the last common ancestor of the five Boraginaceae species in our analysis, and another 3908 orthogroups appear to have duplicated in the last common ancestor of the four known shikonin/alkannin-producing species. Among the 6726 orthogroups duplicated at these internodes is OG0000509, the orthogroup containing <i>AtPPT1</i> and <i>LePPT1</i> (genes involved in the primary metabolic process of ubiquinone biosynthesis) as well as the specialized genes <i>LePGT1</i> and <i>LePGT2</i> involved in shikonin biosynthesis. An additional nine homologous prenyltransferases of unknown function were also identified in the <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genome. This analysis illustrates the importance of gene duplication in the evolution of the shikonin/alkannin pathway, in agreement with other analyses of specialized metabolism in plants (e.g.,<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lopez-Nieves S., et al. Relaxation of tyrosine pathway regulation underlies the evolution of betalain pigmentation in Caryophyllales. New Phytol. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1111/nph.14822&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1711">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Edger, P. P. et al. The butterfly plant arms-race escalated by gene and genome duplications. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 8362â8366 (2015)." href="#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1711_1">44</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Moghe, G. D., Leong, B. J., Hurney, S. M., Jones, A. D. &amp; Last, R. L. Evolutionary routes to biochemical innovation revealed by integrative analysis of a plant-defense related specialized metabolic pathway. Elife 6, e28468 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1714">45</a></sup>). In the case of the <i>PGT</i> and <i>PGT-like</i> sequences in <i>L. erythrorhizon</i>, the initial duplication event appears to have occurred via retrotransposition based on the fact that these sequences appear to have lost all introns (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig3">3a</a>; Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM1">S9</a>). The relative contribution of retrotransposition compared to other processes that generate gene duplicates (e.g., whole-genome duplication) to metabolic innovation in plants remains to be investigated. Overall, this genomic resource will complement the available <i>E. plantagineum</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Tang, C., Li, S., Wang, Y.-T. &amp; Wang, X. Comparative genome/transcriptome analysis probes Boraginalesâ phylogenetic position, WGDs in Boraginales, and key enzyme genes in the alkannin/shikonin core pathway. Mol. Ecol. Resour. 20, 228â241 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1737">20</a></sup> and the extensive sets of transcriptomes (e.g., refs <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wang, S., Ping, L. &amp; Teng, G. Different secondary metabolic responses to MeJA treatment in shikonin-proficient and shikonin-deficient cell lines from Arnebia euchroma (Royle) Johnst. Plant Cell Tiss. Org. 119, 587â598 (2014)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1741">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhu, Y. et al. Involvement of LeMDR, an ATP-binding cassette protein gene, in shikonin transport and biosynthesis in Lithospermum erythrorhizon. BMC Plant Biol. 17, 1â10. (2017)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1741_1">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Wu, F.-Y. et al. Transcriptome analysis explores genes related to shikonin biosynthesis in Lithospermeae plants and provides insights into Boraginales? evolutionary history. Sci. Rep. 7, 4477 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1744">16</a></sup>) and proteomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Takanashi, K. et al. Comparative proteomic analysis of Lithospermum erythrorhizon reveals regulation of a variety of metabolic enzymes leading to comprehensive understanding of the shikonin biosynthetic pathway. Plant Cell Physiol. 60, 19â28 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1748">17</a></sup> published from Boraginaceae species for elucidating the shikonin/alkannin pathway and its evolutionary origin, as well as the evolution of specialized metabolism more generally in this family.</p><p>The connection between shikonin/alkannin and ubiquinone biosynthesis is not limited to homologous prenyltransferases. The hydroxybenzene ring, ring <i>A</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig5">5</a>), of shikonin/alkanninâs naphthazarin moiety is derived from L-phenylalanine via cinnamic acid and 4HBA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Schmid, H. V. &amp; Zenk, M. H. p-hydroxybenzoic acid and mevalonic acid as precursors of the plant naphthoquinone alkannin. Tetrahedron Lett. 12, 4151â4155 (1971)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1761">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Loscher, R. &amp; Heide, L. Biosynthesis of p-hydroxybenzoate from p-coumarate and p-coumaroyl-coenzyme A in cell-free extracts of lithospermum erythrorhizon cell cultures.
