Plant Physiol. 106, 271â279 (1994)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1764">46</a></sup>âthe same route that is partially responsible for forming the benzoquinone ring of ubiquinone (coenzyme Q) in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Block, A. et al. The origin and biosynthesis of the benzenoid moiety of ubiquinone (coenzyme Q) in Arabidopsis. Plant Cell 26, 1938â1948 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1768">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Soubeyrand, E. et al. The peroxidative cleavage of kaempferol contributes to the biosynthesis of the benzenoid moiety of ubiquinone in plants. Plant Cell 30, 2910â2921 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1771">48</a></sup>. Like the shikonin/alkannin pathway, ubiquinone biosynthesis starts off with the conjugation of 4HBA with a polyprenyldiphosphate, catalyzed by a PPT (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig5">5</a>). In addition to the analogous PPT- and PGT-catalyzed reactions, the ubiquinone and shikonin/alkannin pathways share other similar ring modification reactions that occur early in their respective pathways (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig5">5</a>). Both ubiquinone and shikonin/alkannin biosynthesis require the prenylated 4HBA ring to be decarboxylated and hydroxylated at the C1 position, though the sequence of reactions and necessary enzymes are unknown in both pathways. In the <i>Escherichia coli</i> ubiquinone pathway, the non-oxidative ring decarboxylation of 3-polyprenyl-4HBA is catalyzed by UbiD in concert with a UbiX chaperone for substrate reorientation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="White, M. D. et al. UbiX is a flavin prenyltransferase required for bacterial ubiquinone biosynthesis. Nature &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/nature14559&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1785">49</a></sup>. Orthologs of genes encoding UbiD and UbiX are absent from plant genomes. Therefore, if plants also use a series of non-oxidative decarboxylation and hydroxylation steps to modify the C1 position of the 4HBA ring, they do so using other evolved enzymes. It is also possible that plants have evolved to achieve the decarboxylation and hydroxylation via an oxidative decarboxylase that would carry out both reactions. Whatever the mechanism for C1 decarboxylation and hydroxylation, if it is indeed shared between shikonin/alkannin and ubiquinone biosynthesis, it is possible that there is an evolutionary linkage among the enzymes involved given the other metabolic connections between the two pathways.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Similarities between shikonin and ubiquinone biosynthesis."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Similarities between shikonin and ubiquinone biosynthesis.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0301-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_301_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41438-020-0301-9/MediaObjects/41438_2020_301_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1264" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>The committed steps of the shikonin and ubiquinone pathways rely on homologous prenyltransferases that conjugate 4-hydroxybenzoic acid (4HBA), derived from phenylalanine, with a prenyl diphosphate precursor. Subsequent decarboxylation and hydroxylation at the C1 position of the 3-prenylated/geranylated 4HBA ring is required in both pathways, although the responsible enzymes (depicted by â?â) in each route remain unknown. Non-oxidative decarboxylation of the prenylated-4HBA ring is shown as it occurs in bacteria. It is possible that plants use an oxidative decarboxylation mechanism, which would result in concomitant decarboxylation and hydroxylation at the C1 position, bypassing the phenolic intermediates. Abbreviations: GHQH, geranylhydroquinone hydroxylase; PGT, <i>p</i>-hydroxybenzoate:geranyltransferase; PPT, polyprenyltransferase</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Downregulation of <i>LePGT1</i> expression by 95% was sufficient to reduce shikonin production by more than 97% 3âd after transfer of hairy roots to M9 media and darkness (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig4">4b, c</a>). While this provides strong evidence that LePGT1 is principally controlling shikonin formation, it does not rule out that LePGT2 still plays a significant role. Like <i>LePGT1</i>, <i>LePGT2</i> is highly expressed under shikonin producing conditions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig3">3b</a>; Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">9</a>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yazaki, K., Kunihisa, M., Fujisaki, T. &amp; Sato, F. Geranyl diphosphate:4-hydroxybenzoate geranyltransferase from Lithospermum erythrorhizon: cloning and characterization of a key enzyme in shikonin biosynthesis. J. Biol. Chem. 277, 6240â6246 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1834">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Zhu, Y. et al. Involvement of LeMRP, an ATP-binding cassette transporter, in shikonin transport and biosynthesis in Lithospermum erythrorhizon. Plant Biol. 17, 1â9 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1837">50</a></sup>. One explanation for achieving near abolishment of shikonin by only knocking down <i>LePGT1</i> could be that LePGT1 and LePGT2 form heteromers in vivo. Biochemical studies, however, suggest that LePGT1 is capable of functioning homomericly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Ohara, K., Muroya, A., Fukushima, N. &amp; Yazaki, K. Functional characterization of LePGT1, a membrane-bound prenyltransferase involved in the geranylation of p-hydroxybenzoic acid. Biochem J. 421, 231â241 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1844">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Ohara, K., Mito, K. &amp; Yazaki, K. Homogeneous purification and characterization of LePGT1âa membrane-bound aromatic substrate prenyltransferase involved in secondary metabolism of Lithospermum erythrorhizon. FEBS J. 280, 2572â2580 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1847">42</a></sup>, so this appears unlikely. Another possibility is that LePGT1 is the predominant form functioning early in shikonin-producing conditions because of its biochemical properties. LePGT1 has 5- and 10-fold higher affinities for 4HBA and GPP, respectively, compared to LePGT2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Yazaki, K., Kunihisa, M., Fujisaki, T. &amp; Sato, F. Geranyl diphosphate:4-hydroxybenzoate geranyltransferase from Lithospermum erythrorhizon: cloning and characterization of a key enzyme in shikonin biosynthesis. J. Biol. Chem. 277, 6240â6246 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1851">32</a></sup>. It can be envisioned that early in hairy root culture establishment, when precursor concentrations are a priori low, that LePGT1 is the de facto PGT responsible for forming 3-geranyl-4HBA. In this study, shikonin levels were measured at day 3, the first day that visible production occurred in control cultures. It is possible that over time, as precursor pools increase, the relative contribution of LePGT2 would become greater. If any of the PGT-like proteins encoded in the <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genome also contribute PGT activity, their relative contributions would also increase over the culture period. In light of the current study, reports of multiple <i>PGT</i> unigenes and their variable expression patterns in other transcriptomic studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Rai, A. et al. De novo transcriptome assembly and characterization of lithospermum officinale to discover putative genes involved in specialized metabolites biosynthesis. Planta Med. 84, 320â934. (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1862">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wu, F. Y. et al. Transcriptome analysis explores genes related to shikonin biosynthesis in Lithospermeae plants and provides insights into Boraginalesâ evolutionary history. Sci Rep. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/s41598-017-04750-1&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1865">37</a></sup> should be re-investigated to determine if any encode PGT-like proteins. More detailed investigations looking at shikonin formation over time using metabolic flux analysis with stable isotopic labeling are needed to determine the temporal contributions of PGTs.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>The first genome assembly from the medicinally and economically important plant <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> is expected to advance understanding of the evolutionary history of the Boraginales. As just the second genome from a member of this order, the other from <i>E. plantagineum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Tang, C., Li, S., Wang, Y.-T. &amp; Wang, X. Comparative genome/transcriptome analysis probes Boraginalesâ phylogenetic position, WGDs in Boraginales, and key enzyme genes in the alkannin/shikonin core pathway. Mol. Ecol. Resour. 20, 228â241 (2020)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1882">20</a></sup>, the <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genome will provide another piece of the puzzle needed to reconstruct the phylogenetic relationships among the Boraginales, Solanales, Gentianales, and Laminales (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig2">2</a>). The <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genome will also serve as a novel tool for elucidating the remaining missing steps in the shikonin/alkannin pathway and for filling gaps in knowledge about its metabolic origin. Our phylogenetic analysis of prenyltransferases encoded in the <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig3">3a</a>) has already led to the remarkable discovery of several additional encoded PGTs. It also provided evidence to suggest that <i>PGTs</i> arose in a common ancestor of modern shikonin/alkannin-producing Boraginaceae species via a retrotransposition-derived duplication event and subsequent neofunctionalization of an ancestral prenyltransferase gene. Based on homology between PGTs and PPTs it is possible that this points to an evolutionary link between ubiquinone and shikonin/alkannin biosynthesis, especially considering the other metabolic similarities shared between the two pathways (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig5">5</a>). This would not be the first connection found between primary and specialized quinone metabolism in plants as it was recently reported that the pathway to synthesize the naphthoquinone moiety of juglone in black walnut trees (<i>Juglans nigra</i>) is shared with the phylloquinone (vitamin K1) pathway<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="McCoy, R. M., Utturkar, S. M., Crook, J. W., Thimmapuram, J. &amp; Widhalm, J. R. The origin and biosynthesis of the naphthalenoid moiety of juglone in black walnut. Hortic. Res. 5, 67 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1911">51</a></sup>. Taken together, the results from our study provide several new leads for investigating the evolution of specialized metabolism in the Boraginaceae.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Plant materials, growth conditions, and general experimental procedures</h3><p>Seeds of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> (accession Siebold &amp; Zucc.) were obtained from the seed bank at the Leibniz Institute of Plant Genetics and Crop Plant Research (IPK) in Germany and plants were propagated under standard greenhouse conditions to bulk seeds.</p><p>For all hairy root culture work, <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> Siebold &amp; Zucc. seeds were sterilized by chlorine gas according to Lindsey et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Lindsey, I., Rivero, B. E., Calhoun, L. S., Grotewold, C. S. &amp; Brkljacic, E. Standardized method for high-throughput sterilization of Arabidopsis seeds. J. Vis. Exp. 128, 56587 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1936">52</a></sup>. After exposure to chlorine gas for 2âh, seeds were rinsed five times with sterile water and shaken in 200âÂµg/mL cefotaxime overnight. They were then rinsed with sterile water and plated on half-strength Murashige and Skoog (MS, Phytotech Labs) media with 0.1% Plant Preservation Mixture (PPM<sup>TM</sup>, Plant Cell Technology). Seeds were stratified at 4âÂ°C for 2 weeks and moved to room temperature under 12:12 light:dark cycle. Once germinated, seed coats were removed, and each seedling was transferred to a magenta box with half-strength MS media. Plants with their second pairs of true leaves were used in hairy root transformation.</p><p>Hairy roots were maintained in solid Gamborg B5 media (Phytotech Labs) containing 3% sucrose and 10âmg/L Basta (PlantMedia<sup>TM</sup>). Hairy roots were subcultured every two weeks. For quantification of shikonin, 1-cm hairy root fragments were transferred to 20âmL liquid Gamborg B5 media and grown under constant light at 28âÂ°C for two weeks without Basta. Shikonin production was then induced by transferring hairy roots to M9 media<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Fujita, Y., Hara, Y., Ogino, T. &amp; Suga, C. Production of shikonin derivatives by cell suspension cultures of Lithospermum erythrorhizon. Plant Cell Rep. 1, 59â60 (1981)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1947">53</a></sup> and culturing at 28âÂ°C in constant darkness without Basta.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Nanopore sequencing</h3><p>For nanopore sequencing, leaves from an in vitro cultured 3-month-old <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> Siebold &amp; Zucc. plant were frozen in liquid nitrogen, ground by mortar and pestle, and high molecular weight genomic DNA was extracted using a CTAB phenol chloroform extraction protocol (available on protocols.io: <a href="https://doi.org/10.17504/protocols.io.bamnic5e">https://doi.org/10.17504/protocols.io.bamnic5e</a>) and purified using a Genomic DNA Clean and Concentrator kit (Zymo Research). At least 2âÎ¼g of gDNA was used as input for an Oxford Nanopore LSK-109 library ligation kit and sequenced on R9 MinION flow cells. Base calling was performed with Guppy v2.3.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Wick, R. R., Judd, L. M. &amp; Holt, K. E. Performance of neural network basecalling tools for Oxford Nanopore sequencing. Genome Biol. 20, 1â10 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1969">54</a></sup>. Reads less than 3 kilobase pairs long or with quality scores less than 7 were discarded. Reads are available for download at the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (PRJNA596998).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Genome assembly</h3><p><i>Lithospermum erythrorhizon</i> Illumina gDNA PE reads sequenced by Nanjing University in 2018 from an unknown accession were downloaded from NCBI SRA database experiment SRR5644206 and assembled with Abyss v2.1.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Simpson, J. T. et al. ABySS: A parallel assembler for short read sequence data. Genome Res. 19, 1117â1123 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1983">55</a></sup> using a k-mer size of 75. The assembled Abyss contigs and the ONT long reads served as input for the DBG2OLC hybrid assembler using the following parameters: KmerCovTh 2, AdaptiveTh 0.0001, MinOverlap 20, RemoveChimera 1, k 17<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Ye, C., Hill, C. M., Wu, S., Ruan, J. &amp; Ma, Z. DBG2OLC: Efficient assembly of large genomes using long erroneous reads of the third generation sequencing technologies. Sci. Rep. 6, 1â9 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1987">21</a></sup>. The resulting hybrid assembly was error corrected via five rounds of polishing with the Illumina gDNA reads using Pilon v1.23<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pone.&#xA;                  &#xA;                &#xA;&#xA;                  0112963&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1991">56</a></sup>. Five additional rounds of Pilon polishing were performed using <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> Siebold &amp; Zucc. stranded RNA-seq reads (see RNA-seq experiments section below) as input to fix single nucleotide errors in transcribed regions. Genome size was estimated using GenomeScope<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Vurture, G. W. et al. GenomeScope: Fast reference-free genome profiling from short reads. Bioinformatics 33, 2202â2204 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d65884e1998">57</a></sup> with a k-mer (kâ=â21) depth distribution of the Illumina gDNA reads calculated using Jellyfish v2.2.10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="MarÃ§ais, G. &amp; Kingsford, C. A fast, lock-free approach for efficient parallel counting of occurrences of k-mers. Bioinformatics 27, 764â770 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2003">58</a></sup>. Illumina gDNA reads were mapped back to the final assembly using BWA v0.7.15<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Li, H. Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Preprint at &#xA;                  https://arxiv.org/abs/1303.3997&#xA;                  &#xA;                ." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2007">59</a></sup>, RNAseq reads were mapped using STAR v2.5.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Dobin, A. et al. STAR: Ultrafast universal RNA-seq aligner. Bioinformatics 29, 15â21 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2011">60</a></sup>, and ONT reads were mapped using minimap2 v2.13<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Li, H. Minimap and miniasm: Fast mapping and de novo assembly for noisy long sequences. Bioinformatics 32, 2103â2110 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2015">61</a></sup>. Histograms of Illumina and ONT read depth when mapped to the final assembly were generated by the program purge_haplotigs v1.1.1 to assess the level of assembly heterozygosity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Roach, M. J., Schmidt, S. A. &amp; Borneman, A. R. Purge Haplotigs: Allelic contig reassignment for third-gen diploid genome assemblies. BMC Bioinforma. 19, 1â10. (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2019">62</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Genome annotation</h3><p>De novo repeat identification was performed using RepeatModeler v1.0.9 and masked using RepeatMasker v4.0.7 (<a href="http://repeatmasker.org">http://repeatmasker.org</a>). Gene model and protein prediction was conducted with MAKER2 v2.31.10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Holt C., Yandell M. MAKER2: An annotation pipeline and genome-database management tool for second-generation genome projects. BMC Bioinforma. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2105-12-491&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2038">63</a></sup> by supplying protein homology-based evidence, transcriptomic evidence in the form of a genome guided transcriptome assembly generated from in-house Siebold &amp; Zucc. stranded RNA-seq reads (see RNA-seq experiments section below) using Trinity v2.5.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Haas, B. J. et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat. Protoc. 8, 1494â1512 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2042">64</a></sup>, and ab initio gene calling using SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinforma. 5, 1â9 (2004)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2046">65</a></sup> and BRAKER2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Hoff, K. J., Lomsadze, A., Borodovsky, M., &amp; Stanke M. in Gene Prediction: Methods and Protocols (ed Kollmar, M.). 65â95 (Springer New York: New York, NY, 2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2050">66</a></sup>. Gene models with an AED score &lt;0.9 or ones that encoded a predicted protein &lt;30 amino acids long were excluded from the final gene set. Additional information on the full annotation pipeline is presented in Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM6">6</a>. Conservation of core genes was performed using BUSCO v2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Waterhouse, R. M. et al. BUSCO applications from quality assessments to gene prediction and phylogenomics. Mol. Biol. Evol. 35, 543â548 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2058">26</a></sup>. Functional annotation of the final protein set was performed using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Jones, P. et al. InterProScan 5: genome-scale protein function classification. Bioinformatics 30, 1236â1240 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2062">22</a></sup> and TargetP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Armenteros, J. J. A. et al. Detecting sequence signals in targeting peptides using deep learning. Life Sci. Alliance &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.26508/lsa.201900429&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2066">67</a></sup>.</p><p>We checked the genome for possible contamination using the Alien Index (AI) pipeline (<a href="https://github.rcac.purdue.edu/jwisecav/phylo-pipe">https://github.rcac.purdue.edu/jwisecav/phylo-pipe</a>; last updated August 26, 2019) as previously described<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wisecaver, J. H., Alexander, W. G., King, S. B., Todd Hittinger, C., Rokas, A. Dynamic evolution of nitric oxide detoxifying flavohemoglobins, a family of single-protein metabolic modules in bacteria and eukaryotes. Mol. Biol. Evol. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msw073&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2080">27</a></sup>. Briefly, each predicted protein sequence was queried against the NCBI RefSeq database (release 97) using Diamond v0.9.22.123<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Buchfink, B., Xie, C. &amp; Huson, D. H. Fast and sensitive protein alignment using DIAMOND. Nat. Methods 12, 59â60 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2084">68</a></sup>, and the AI score was calculated based on the output. The AI score is given by the formula: AIâ=ânbsO-nbsE, where <i>nbsO</i> is the normalized bit score of the best hit to a species outside of the eudicot lineage, <i>nbsE</i> is the normalized bit score of the best hit to a species within the eudicot lineage (skipping all hits to <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> present in the RefSeq database). AI scores range from -1 to 1, being greater than zero if the predicted protein sequence had a better hit to a non-eudicot species, suggestive of either HGT or contamination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Wisecaver, J. H., Alexander, W. G., King, S. B., Todd Hittinger, C., Rokas, A. Dynamic evolution of nitric oxide detoxifying flavohemoglobins, a family of single-protein metabolic modules in bacteria and eukaryotes. Mol. Biol. Evol. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/molbev/msw073&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2098">27</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">RNA-seq experiments</h3><p>For the <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> root periderm and vascular tissues RNA-seq experiment, 3-month-old Siebold &amp; Zucc. plants grown in soil under standard greenhouse conditions were harvested. Roots were collected from nine individual plants and divided into three groups, each containing three unique individuals. The periderm and vascular tissues were isolated by peeling the periderm from the roots (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM5">5a</a>), and the prepared portions from the three individuals in each group were pooled. Tissues were frozen in liquid nitrogen, ground by mortar and pestle, and 100âmg was used to analyze total shikonin content each sample (Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM5">5b</a>). From the same sets of samples, RNA was extracted as described below, quantified, and DNase-treated (NEB) according to the manufacturerâs instructions. A total of six cDNA libraries from the three biological replicates prepared from each of the <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> periderm and vascular tissue pools, were constructed using a ribominus TruSeq Stranded Total RNA library prep kit (Illumina, San Diego, CA), and 101-bp paired-end reads were generated via Illumina HiSeq 2500 at the Purdue Genomics Center, with at least 67 million reads per library. Sequence quality was assessed by FastQC (v. 0.10.0; <a href="http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk">http://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk</a>). The raw data were submitted to the Sequence Read Archive (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/</a>) and are available at the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (<a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA596998">PRJNA596998</a>).</p><p>The experimental design for the RNA-seq experiment comparing <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> hairy roots sampled in B5 in the light and M9 in the dark was based on a previous report of observed rapid increases in expression of shikonin precursor pathway genes, and in <i>PGT</i>, within 2âh after switching <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> cell cultures from growth in B5 in the light to growth in M9 in darkness<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zhang, W. J. et al. Expression analysis of shikonin-biosynthetic genes in response to M9 medium and light in Lithospermum erythrorhizon cell cultures. Plant Cell Tissue Organ Cult. 101, 135â142 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2157">40</a></sup>. In this study, several cultures from three independently generated <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> hairy root lines were started in liquid Gamborg B5 media containing 3% sucrose at 28âÂ°C in the light (~100âÂµEâm<sup>â2</sup>âs<sup>â1</sup>). After 2 weeks, hairy roots from three cultures for each of the three lines (<i>n</i>â=â3 biological replicates per line) were harvested and pooled to represent the B5 light-treated samples. The remaining hairy root cultures were transferred to M9 media and darkness. After 2âh, hairy roots from three cultures for each of the three lines (<i>n</i>â=â3 biological replicates per line) were harvested and pooled to represent the M9 dark-treated samples. Samples were frozen in liquid nitrogen, ground by mortar and pestle, and RNA was extracted as described below. Six cDNA libraries were generated with a TruSeq Stranded mRNA library prep kit (Illumina, San Diego, CA) and were sequenced on an Illumina NovaSeq 6000 at the Purdue Genomics Center. Sequence quality assessment were performed as described above for the periderm and vascular tissues RNA-seq experiment. The raw data were submitted to the Sequence Read Archive (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra/</a>) and are available at the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (PRJNA596998).</p><p>Additionally, unstranded RNA-seq data of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> whole roots and aerial tissue from an unknown accession was downloaded from the NCBI SRA (experiments SRR3957230 and SRR3957231) to include in the gene expression analysis. Gene abundance estimates of PGT and PGT-like genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig3">3b</a>, Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">9</a>) were measured using Kallisto v0.45.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Bray, N. L., Pimentel, H., Melsted, P. &amp; Pachter, L. Near-optimal probabilistic RNA-seq quantification. Nat. Biotechnol. 34, 525â527 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2194">69</a></sup> and normalized for library depth using DESeq2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Love, M. I., Huber, W. &amp; Anders, S. Moderated estimation of fold change and dispersion for RNA-seq data with DESeq2. Genome Biol. 15, 550 (2014)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2198">70</a></sup>. Differential expression status was determined using the EdgeR v3.24.3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Robinson, M. D., McCarthy, D. J. &amp; Smyth, G. K. edgeR: A Bioconductor package for differential expression analysis of digital gene expression data. Bioinformatics 26, 139â140 (2009)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2203">71</a></sup> package. For the EdgeR analysis, raw counts were normalized into effective library sizes using the trimmed mean of M-values (TMM) method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Robinson, M. D., Oshlack A. A scaling normalization method for differential expression analysis of RNA-seq data. Genome Biol. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1186/gb-2010-11-3-r25&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2207">72</a></sup>, and exact tests were conducted using a trended dispersion value and a double tail reject region. A false discovery rate was calculated using the BenjaminiâHochberg procedure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. R. Stat. Soc. Ser. B 57, 289â300 (1995)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2211">73</a></sup>. Genes with a log<sub>2</sub>-fold change in abundance greater than 1 and false discovery rate less than 0.05 were considered as differentially represented.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">De novo transcriptome assemblies of additional Boraginaceae species</h3><p>Illumina RNA-seq reads from additional shikonin-producing species <i>L. officinale</i>, <i>Arnebia euchroma</i> and <i>Echium plantagineum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Rai, A. et al. De novo transcriptome assembly and characterization of lithospermum officinale to discover putative genes involved in specialized metabolites biosynthesis. Planta Med. 84, 320â934. (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2233">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Wu, F. Y. et al. Transcriptome analysis explores genes related to shikonin biosynthesis in Lithospermeae plants and provides insights into Boraginalesâ evolutionary history. Sci Rep. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/s41598-017-04750-1&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2236">37</a></sup> were downloaded from the following NCBI SRA experiments: SRR4034889, SRR4034892, SRR4034890, SRR4034891, SRR6799516, SRR6799517, and SRR6799518. Raw RNA-seq reads were normalized and error corrected with BBnorm using a target size of 40 and a minimum depth of 2 (software last modified October 19, 2017) and Tadpole using default parameters (software last modified June 27, 2017), programs from the BBMap software package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Bushnel, l B. BBTools Software Package (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2240">74</a></sup>. The resulting clean reads were assembled de novo using Trinity v2.5.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Haas, B. J. et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat. Protoc. 8, 1494â1512 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2244">64</a></sup> with default parameters for stranded (<i>L. officinale</i>) and unstranded (<i>E. plantagineum</i> and <i>A. euchroma</i>) libraries. Coding regions were inferred using TransDecoder v3.0.1<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Haas, B. TransDecoder (Find Coding Regions within Transcripts) &#xA;                  http://transdecoder.sourceforge.net&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2258">75</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Identification of orthologous gene families</h3><p>Homology between the predicted proteomes of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> and 31 other eudicot species was determined with OrthoFinder v2.1.2 using the following parameters: -S diamond -M msa -T fasttree<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 1â14. (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2273">28</a></sup>. The species tree in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig2">2</a> was generated using TreeGraph2 v2.15.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="StÃ¶ver, B. C. &amp; MÃ¼ller K. F. TreeGraph 2: Combining and visualizing evidence from different phylogenetic analyses. BMC Bioinformatics &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1186/1471-2105-11-7&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2010)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2280">76</a></sup>. Hypergeometric tests were performed in python using the SciPy library hypergeom, and <i>p</i>-values were adjusted for multiple comparisons using the StatsModels library multitest with the Benjamini &amp; Hochberg (BH) method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Benjamini, Y. &amp; Hochberg, Y. Controlling the false discovery rate: a practical and powerful approach to multiple testing. J. R. Stat. Soc. Ser. B 57, 289â300 (1995)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2288">73</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>We performed a separate phylogenetic analysis of the orthogroup containing LePGT1 and LePGT2 (OG0000509). Three sequences were excluded due to their long branches, including the suspected pseudogene Leryth_015069. The remaining sequences were aligned with MAFFT v7.407 using the E-INS-I strategy and following parameters: --maxiterate 1000 --bl 45 --op 1.0 --retree 3<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Standley, D. M. MAFFT multiple sequence alignment software version 7: Improvements in performance and usability. Mol. Biol. Evol. 30, 772â780 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2300">77</a></sup>. The maximum likelihood phylogeny was constructed using IQ-TREE version 1.6.10<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Nguyen, L. T., Schmidt, H. A., Von Haeseler, A. &amp; Minh, B. Q. IQ-TREE: A fast and effective stochastic algorithm for estimating maximum-likelihood phylogenies. Mol. Biol. Evol. 32, 268â274 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2304">78</a></sup> using the built in ModelFinder to determine the best-fit substitution model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Kalyaanamoorthy, S., Minh,B. Q., Wong, T. K. F., Von Haeseler, A. &amp; Jermiin L. S. ModelFinder: fast model selection for accurate phylogenetic estimates. Nat Methods. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1038/nmeth.4285&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2017)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2308">79</a></sup> and performing SH-aLRT and ultrafast bootstrapping analyses with 1,000 replicates each. The gene tree in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#Fig3">3a</a> was generated using iTOL v4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Letunic, I. &amp; Bork, P. Interactive Tree Of Life (iTOL) v4: recent updates and new developments. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1093/nar/gkz239&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2019)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2315">80</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Synteny analysis</h3><p>Regions of shared synteny within the genome of <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> were detected using SynMap2 on the online Comparative Genomics Platform (CoGe) using default settings with the exception that the merge syntenic blocks algorithm was set to Quota Align Merge, the syntenic depth algorithm was set to Quota Align, and the CodeML option was activated to calculate substitution rates between syntenic CDS pairs. For syntenic blocks containing PGT genes and their homologs, the encompasing contigs were aligned using promer (v3.07) of the MUMmer4 alignment system<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="MarÃ§ais, G. et al. MUMmer4: A fast and versatile genome alignment system. PLoS Comput Biol. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1371/journal.pcbi.1005944&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2330">81</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Cloning and generation of LePGT1i hairy root lines</h3><p>For the <i>LePGT1</i>-RNAi construct, ï»¿DNA containing two spliced <i>LePGT1</i> cDNA fragments of the coding region corresponding to nucleotides 179-698 and 179-502, the latter in antisense orientation to create a hairpin structure, was synthesized (Genscript, Piscataway, NJ). 5â-CACC was added for subcloning into ï»¿pENTR<sup>TM</sup>/D-TOPO (Invitrogen<sup>TM</sup>, Carlsbad, CA), sequence verified, and transferred into the destination vector, pB2GW7<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Karimi, M., InzÃ©, D. &amp; Depicker, A. GATEWAY vectors for Agrobacterium-mediated plant transformation. Trends Plant Sci. 7, 193â195 (2002)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2352">82</a></sup>, by recombination using LR Clonase Enzyme Mix<sup>TM</sup> (Invitrogen). The final construct, pB2GW7<i>-PGT1i</i>, was transformed into <i>Agrobacterium rhizogenes</i> strain ATCC 15834 competent cells by freeze-thaw transformation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Cui, W., Liu, W. &amp; Wu, G. A simple method for the transformation of Agrobacterium tumefaciens by foreign DNA. Chin. J. Biotechnol. 11, 267â274 (1995)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2365">83</a></sup>. Briefly, competent cells were incubated with 100âng of pB2GW7-<i>PGT1i</i> for 15âmin on ice. Then, cells were snap frozen in liquid nitrogen for 5âmin and consecutively thawed at 37âÂ°C for 5âmin. Nutrient Broth (NB) media was added to the culture and kept in 37âÂ°C with shaking for 2âh before being plated on NB agar containing 50âÂµg/mL spectinomycin for selection.</p><p><i>L. erythrorhizon</i> hairy root <i>PGT1i</i> lines were generated based on the protocol from Fang et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Fang, R. et al. Transgenic analysis reveals LeACS-1 as a positive regulator of ethylene-induced shikonin biosynthesis in Lithospermum erythrorhizon hairy roots. Plant Mol. Biol. &#xA;                  https://doi.org/10.1007/s11103-015-0421-z&#xA;                  &#xA;                 (2016)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2380">84</a></sup> with slight modification. <i>A. rhizogenes</i> containing pB2GW7<i>-PGT1i</i> was inoculated in NB with 50âÂµg/mL spectinomycin and kept in shaking incubator at 28âÂ°C until reached OD<sub>600</sub>â=â1. Then, acetosyringone was added to the media to a final concentration of 0.1âmM and the culture was grown further for 4âh in dark. The <i>A. rhizogenes</i> was then pelleted by centrifugation. The pellet was washed and resuspended in half-strength MS containing 0.1âmM acetosyringone. Each stem of sterile plants grown in tissue culture was wounded by surgical blade and the prepared culture was applied to the wounded area by cotton swab. The plants were then kept in darkness for 1âd and returned to normal growth conditions. The hairy roots emerged between 10 and 28âd post infection. Emergent roots were excised and placed on Gamborg B5 media with 3% sucrose and 200âÂµg/mL cefotaxime to eliminate <i>A</i>. <i>rhizogenes</i>. After 2 weeks, hairy roots were transferred to Gamborg B5 media containing 3% sucrose and 10âmg/L Basta for selection for 2 weeks. Hairy root lines transformed by <i>A. rhizogenes</i> without pB2GW7-<i>PGT1i</i> were generated to use as control.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">RNA extraction and qRT-PCR</h3><p>Total RNA was extracted from ~20âmg of hairy root tissues according to the protocol from Ghawana et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Ghawana, S. et al. An RNA isolation system for plant tissues rich in secondary metabolites. BMC Res. Notes 4, 85 (2011)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2417">85</a></sup> Briefly, the samples were frozen by liquid nitrogen and ground by mortar and pestle. 2âmL of RNA extraction buffer (phenol containing 0.1% sodium dodecyl sulfate, 0.32âM sodium acetate, and 0.1âM ethylenediaminetetra acetic acid) was added to the sample in the mortar and mixed, followed by addition 0.8âmL of RNAse-free water. After mixing, the mixture was incubated for 5âmin before transfer to microtubes. 0.3âmL of chloroform was added, and the sample was vortexed and centrifuged at 4âÂ°C, 13,000ârpm for 10âmin. The supernatant was then transferred to the new tube containing 0.6âmL isopropanol. Next, the sample was mixed by inverting, and nucleic acids were precipitated at â20âÂ°C for 10âmin. After precipitation, the sample was centrifuged at 4âÂ°C for 10âmin. The pellet was washed with 70% ethanol before air drying. The RNA pellet was dissolved in RNase-free water. The total RNA was concentrated and purified using an RNA Clean &amp; Concentrator Kit (Zymo Research) with on-column DNase treatment (Zymo Research) using the manufacturer protocol. cDNA synthesis was performed by 5X All-In-One RT MasterMix (abm) according to the manufacturer instructions using 500âng of total RNA.</p><p>Expression of <i>LePGT1</i> and <i>LePGT2</i> was measured by qRT-PCR with comparative quantification using the 2<sup>âÎÎCT</sup> method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Livak, K. J. &amp; Schmittgen, T. D. Analysis of relative gene expression data using real-time quantitative PCR and the 2-ÎÎCT method. Methods 25, 402â408 (2001)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2432">86</a></sup>. Primers were designed using Primer-BLAST on NCBI<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="Ye, J. et al. Primer-BLAST: a tool to design target-specific primers for polymerase chain reaction. BMC Bioinforma. 13, 134 (2012)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2436">87</a></sup>. Due to the sequence similarity of members of the <i>LePGT</i> and <i>LePGT-like</i> (Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">9</a>) gene family, each primer was checked against all members for possible off-target matches. To minimize off-target amplification, primer pairs were selected that had either (i) four or more mismatches in a primer to all other <i>LePGT</i> and <i>LePGT-like</i> family genes or (ii) two mismatches in one primer and three mismatches in the other primer to all other <i>LePGT</i> and <i>LePGT-like</i> family genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Lefever, S., Pattyn, F., Hellemans, J. &amp; Vandesompele, J. Single-nucleotide polymorphisms and other mismatches reduce performance of quantitative PCR assays. Clin. Chem. 59, 1470â1480 (2013)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2463">88</a></sup>. qRT-PCR reactions were performed using a QuantStudio<sup>TM</sup> 6 (ThermoFisher) in a 10âÎ¼L reaction as follows: 5âÎ¼L of 5x Fast SYBR Green PCR master mix (ThermoFisher), 1âÎ¼L each of the forward and reverse primers (50â900ânM final concentration; Table S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM7">12</a>), and 3âÎ¼L of diluted cDNA. Expression was normalized to <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> glyceraldehyde 3-phosphate dehydrogenase (<i>LeGAPDH</i>) using primers from Zhao et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Zhao, H. et al. Overexpression of LeMYB1 enhances shikonin formation by up-regulating key shikonin biosynthesis-related genes in Lithospermum erythrorhizon. Biol. Plant 59, 429â435 (2015)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2479">89</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Shikonin extraction and quantification</h3><p>The extraction of total shikonins was modified from Boehm et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Boehm, R., Sommer, S., Li, S.-M. &amp; Heide, L. Genetic engineering on shikonin biosynthesis: expression of the bacterial ubiA gene in Lithospermum erythrorhizon. Plant Cell Physiol. 41, 911â919 (2000)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2491">90</a></sup>. Briefly, a 4âmL sample of growth media from each hairy root line was sampled at day 3 after transfer to M9 and darkness and extracted with 4âmL of chloroform. The chloroform layer was separated and dried under a gentle stream of N<sub>2</sub> at 40âÂ°C. Base hydrolysis was performed on the remaining residue in the tube by adding 2âmL of 1âM NaOH and shaking for 1âh at room temperature. The solution was neutralized by adding 1âmL of 6âM HCl and vortexed. Shikonin was extracted by adding 3âmL of ethyl acetate by liquidâliquid extraction. The ethyl acetate layer was separated and dried under N<sub>2</sub>, dissolved in 250âÂµL methanol, and 20âÂµL was used for detection by high performance liquid chromatography with diode array detection (HPLC-DAD). The extraction procedure was performed in reduced light to minimize the photo-degradation of shikonin.</p><p>HPLC-DAD analyses were performed with an Agilent 1260 Infinity HPLC system (Agilent Technologies). Chromatographic separation of shikonin was achieved using a Zorbax SB-C18 column (4.6âÃâ250âmm, Agilent) kept at 25âÂ°C. The mobile phase gradient started at 60% A (30:70 acetonitrile and water with 0.1% formic acid) and 40% B (30:70 isopropanol and acetonitrile with 0.1% formic acid) with 1âmin hold and then linearly increased to 99% B over 15âmin with a hold of 4âmin, and then returned to 40% B from 19 to 20âmin with a hold of 1âmin. Shikonin eluted at 8.4âmin and was detected at 520ânm by DAD. Instrument operation and data analysis steps were performed through the Agilent ChemStation software. Shikonin quantitation by DAD was done by running a linear range of 1.25, 2.5, 5, 10, and 20ânmol calibration standards, followed by a linear regression formula calculation. Differences between shikonin content in each line (<i>n</i>â=â3â4 biological replicates) was analyzed using one-way ANOVA and the means were compared with Tukeyâs HSD post hoc test at 95% significant level.</p><p>For analysis of shikonins from <i>L. erythrorhizon</i> root periderm and vascular tissues for RNA-seq, HPLC coupled with fluorescence detection (HPLC-FLD; Fig. S<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#MOESM5">5</a>), chromatographic separation was conducted using a Zorbax Eclipse XDB-C18 column (4.6âÃâ150âmm, Agilent). The column was eluted at 25âÂ°C using a linear gradient starting from 70% A (water with 0.1% formic acid) and 30% B (acetonitrile with 0.1% formic acid), to 1% A and 99% B over 40âmin at a flow rate of 0.5âmLâmin<sup>â1</sup>, followed by a 10âmin re-equilibration step. Shikonin eluted at 25.9âmin and was detected by fluorescence using Î»<sub>ex</sub>â=â228ânm and Î»<sub>em</sub>â=â390ânm after passing ï»¿through an in-line post-column dry reactor packed with zinc dust, which was previously used for detection of the 1,4-naphthoquinone juglone<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="McCoy, R. M., Utturkar, S. M., Crook, J. W., Thimmapuram, J. &amp; Widhalm, J. R. The origin and biosynthesis of the naphthalenoid moiety of juglone in black walnut. Hortic. Res. 5, 67 (2018)." href="/articles/s41438-020-0301-9#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d65884e2521">51</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section>
